## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: classical mechanics (nonlinear dynamics) — nonlinear oscillators
**Analysis**: The target dv_dt is acceleration expressed as a function of position x, velocity v, and possibly explicit time t, matching second-order oscillator equations x' = v and v' = dv_dt. Common structures combine restoring forces (functions of x), damping/energy injection (functions of v and x), and external/parametric forcing (functions of t).

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Kinematic state-space form: dv_dt = f(x, v, t) with dx_dt = v (generic nonlinear oscillator model)
- Linear harmonic oscillator: dv_dt = -c0 * x (restoring force proportional to displacement)
- Viscously damped oscillator: dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * v (linear stiffness + linear damping)
- Driven damped oscillator: dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * v + c2 * cos(c3 * t + c4) (periodic external forcing)
- Parametrically excited oscillator (Mathieu-type): dv_dt = -(c0 + c1 * cos(c2 * t)) * x - c3 * v (time-periodic stiffness modulation)
- Duffing oscillator (cubic stiffness): dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * x**3 - c2 * v (hardening/softening spring nonlinearity)
- Nonlinear damping (quadratic drag): dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * v * Abs(v) (velocity-squared dissipation, e.g., aerodynamic drag)
- Rayleigh oscillator (self-excited via velocity nonlinearity): dv_dt = -c0 * x + c1 * v - c2 * v**3 (negative linear damping stabilized by cubic damping)
- van der Pol oscillator (self-excited via state-dependent damping): dv_dt = -c0 * x + c1 * (c2 - x**2) * v (amplitude-limited oscillations via x-dependent damping)
- Pendulum-type restoring force: dv_dt = -c0 * sin(c1 * x) - c2 * v (nonlinear restoring force from geometry; x interpreted as angle)
- Dry/Coulomb friction (nonsmooth): dv_dt = -c0 * x - c1 * tanh(c2 * v) (approximately constant-magnitude friction opposing motion)
- Liénard-type polynomial damping: dv_dt = -c0 * x - (c1 + c2 * x**2 + c3 * x**4) * v (general oscillator class including many limit-cycle models)
- Time-dependent damping (modulated losses): dv_dt = -c0 * x - (c1 + c2 * cos(c3 * t)) * v (periodic dissipation/feedback modulation)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Conservative restoring forces imply dv_dt depends primarily on x and is odd in x near equilibrium (e.g., -x, -sin(x)); even-power terms in the potential generate odd-power terms in dv_dt (e.g., x^4 potential -> -x^3 force).
- Dissipative forces typically oppose motion, so damping terms are odd in v (e.g., -v, -v|v|, -tanh(v)); this creates phase-space spiral-in behavior toward an attractor.
- Self-excited/limit-cycle oscillators often show negative damping at small amplitude but positive damping at large amplitude, captured by factors like (c0 - x**2)*v or v - v^3.
- External forcing appears as additive time-periodic terms (sin/cos in t) that can produce resonance peaks and phase locking; the forcing does not vanish at x=v=0.
- Parametric excitation typically multiplies x (or v) by a periodic function of t (e.g., (c0 + c1*cos(c2*t))*x), leading to instability tongues and amplitude growth at specific modulation frequencies.
- Geometric nonlinearities often replace linear -x with -sin(c*x) (pendulum-like), yielding amplitude-dependent frequency and bounded restoring acceleration.
- Nonlinear drag in fluids/large Reynolds number regimes is better modeled by v*Abs(v) rather than v, producing stronger damping at high speeds and sharper waveform decay.
- Nonsmooth friction (Coulomb) introduces near-discontinuous dependence on v (sign(v)); smooth approximations like tanh(c*v) mimic stick-slip tendencies without explicit piecewise logic.

I need to compare each candidate against the Ground Truth (GT):

**GT numerical expression:** `-0.752289977167006*v*(1 - x**2) - 0.61401597703647*v - 0.307007988518235*v*exp(-Abs(x)) - 1.1598282332023*x**3`

Since x is strictly positive on this benchmark, `Abs(x) = x`, so:
`-0.752289977167006*v + 0.752289977167006*v*x**2 - 0.61401597703647*v - 0.307007988518235*v*exp(-x) - 1.1598282332023*x**3`

Combining v terms:
`= -1.1598282332023*x**3 + 0.752289977167006*v*x**2 + (-0.752289977167006 - 0.61401597703647)*v - 0.307007988518235*v*exp(-x)`
`= -1.1598282332023*x**3 + 0.752289977167006*v*x**2 - 1.366305954203476*v - 0.307007988518235*v*exp(-x)`

So GT has these basis functions:
1. `x**3`
2. `v*x**2`
3. `v`
4. `v*exp(-x)` (since x > 0, this is `v*exp(-Abs(x))`)

No constant offset term (the expression equals 0 when v=0 and x=0).

Now I'll check each candidate for whether it can match these four basis functions exactly.

**Key requirement:** The candidate must be able to produce the term `v*exp(-x)`. This is the critical distinguishing feature.

Let me check which candidates can produce `v*exp(-x)`:

- Most candidates have polynomial terms in x and v, plus various extra terms.
- The term `v*exp(-x)` requires a specific functional form.

**Candidate 1:** `c0*x**3 + c1 + c2*v*x**2 + c3*v*x + c4*v + c5*v*(c6 + x)**c7 + c8*v*exp(c9*t)`
